{"Status": "Page", "Paper Overview": {"type_of_reference": "JOUR", "Title": "A Hubble Space Telescope lensing survey of X-ray luminous galaxy clusters - III. A multiply imaged extremely red galaxy at z=1.6", "authors": ["Smith, Graham P.", "Smail, Ian", "Kneib, J. -P.", "Davis, C. J.", "Takamiya, M.", "Ebeling, H.", "Czoske, O."], "journal_name": "Monthly Notices of the Royal Astronomical Society", "volume": "333", "publication_year": "2002/06/1", "start_page": "L16", "end_page": "L20", "keywords": ["gravitational lensing", "galaxies: clusters: individual: A68  galaxies: evolution", "galaxies: high-redshift", "galaxies: individual: EROJ003707+0909.5  galaxies: spiral", "Astrophysics"], "url": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2002MNRAS.333L..16S", "abstract": "We present near-infrared spectroscopy and Hubble Space Telescope (HST) imaging of galaxy J003707+0909.5, the brightest of three gravitationally lensed images of an extremely red object (ERO) at z=1.6, in the field of the massive cluster A68 (z=0.255). We exploit the superlative resolution of our HST data and the enhanced spatial resolution and sensitivity afforded by the lens amplification to reconstruct the source-plane properties of this ERO. Our morphological and photometric analysis reveals that EROJ003707 is an L<SUP>*</SUP> early-type disc galaxy and we estimate that ~10 per cent of galaxies with (R-K)>=5.3 and K<=21 may have similar properties. The unique association of passive EROs with elliptical galaxies therefore appears to be too simplistic. We speculate on the evolution of EROJ003707: if gas continues to cool on to this galaxy in the manner predicted by hierarchical galaxy formation models, then by the present day, EROJ003707 could evolve into a very luminous spiral galaxy.", "doi": "10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x", "custom1": "eprint: arXiv:astro-ph/0203402", "issn": "0035-8711", "number": "1"}, "ADS link": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2002MNRAS.333L..16S", "Publisher link via ADS gateway": "https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/link_gateway/2002MNRAS.333L..16S/PUB_HTML", "Action": "Loaded page", "Paper text": "\r\n\r\n\r\n<!DOCTYPE html>\r\n<html lang=\"en\" class=\"no-js\">\r\n\r\n<head>\r\n\r\n        <title>Hubble Space Telescope lensing survey of X-ray luminous galaxy clusters \u2013 III. type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnras\" /> \r\n    </div>\r\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\r\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562862 id=\"20562862\" class=\"abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We present near-infrared spectroscopy and <em>Hubble Space Telescope (HST)</em> imaging of galaxy J003707+0909.5, the brightest of three gravitationally lensed images of an extremely red object (ERO) at <em>z</em>=1.6, in the field of the massive cluster A 68 (<em>z</em>=0.255). We exploit the superlative resolution of our <em>HST</em> data and the enhanced spatial resolution and sensitivity afforded by the lens amplification to reconstruct the source-plane properties of this ERO. Our morphological and photometric analysis reveals that ERO J003707 is an <em>L</em><sup>\u2605</sup> early-type disc galaxy and we estimate that \u223c10 per cent of galaxies with (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3 and <em>K</em>\u226421 may have similar properties. The unique association of passive EROs with elliptical galaxies therefore appears to be too simplistic. We speculate on the evolution of ERO J003707: if gas continues to cool on to this galaxy in the manner predicted by hierarchical galaxy formation models, then by the present day, ERO J003707 could evolve into a very luminous spiral galaxy.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing&quot;\">gravitational lensing</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: clusters: individual: A 68&quot;\">galaxies: clusters: individual: A 68</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: evolution&quot;\">galaxies: evolution</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: high-redshift&quot;\">galaxies: high-redshift</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: individual: ERO J003707+0909.5&quot;\">galaxies: individual: ERO J003707+0909.5</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: spiral&quot;\">galaxies: spiral</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562864 id=\"20562864\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss0>1 Introduction</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The cores of massive galaxy clusters act as powerful gravitational lenses, providing a magnified view of serendipitously placed distant galaxies. Indeed, close alignment of a background galaxy behind a cluster lens can lead to multiple images being visible. This phenomenon, known as strong lensing, has been applied on numerous occasions to the study of high-redshift galaxies (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Hammer &amp; Rigaut 1989</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Ebbels et al. 1996</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Franx et al. 1997</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Seitz et al. 1998</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Pell\u00f3 et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Ellis et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001a</a>). A key feature of these studies is that the lens magnification enables intrinsically low-luminosity galaxies at remote epochs to be observed and studied in great detail.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">One class of distant galaxy that may benefit from the enhanced sensitivity provided by a gravitational lens is extremely red objects (EROs). The defining optical/near-infrared colours of EROs, (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3, should select either distant elliptical galaxies (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Dunlop et al. 1996</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Soifer et al. 1999</a>) or heavily dust-obscured systems at <em>z</em>\u223c1\u20132 (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Dey et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Pierre et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Afonso et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001b</a>). Here we concentrate on those EROs whose (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colour is dominated by the 4000-\u00c5 break of an evolved stellar population (i.e. \u2018passive EROs\u2019). Various authors (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kauffmann &amp; Charlot 1998</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Fontana et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Daddi et al. 2000</a>) have used observations of passive EROs and other <em>K</em>-selected samples to argue for or against monolithic collapse (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Eggen, Lynden-Bell &amp; Sandage 1962</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Larson 1975</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Tinsley &amp; Gunn 1976</a>) or hierarchical assembly (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">White &amp; Frenk 1991</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Cole et al. 2000</a>) theories of galaxy formation. Regardless of the details of this debate, both arguments implicitly assume that <em>all</em> passive EROs are elliptical galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The importance of EROs to our understanding of galaxy formation and the intrinsic faintness of these systems (<em>R</em>\u227323, <em>K</em>\u227318) recently motivated us to search for EROs in the fields of massive gravitational lenses (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2002\u2013 S02a</a>). In this letter, we present near-infrared spectroscopy of ERO J003707+0909.5, drawn from S02a's sample. We describe our observations in Section 2, present our lens modelling in Section 3, describe our source-plane analysis in Section 4, discuss the diversity of passive EROs in Section 5, speculate on the evolution of ERO J003707 in Section 6 and finally summarize our conclusions in Section 7. We assume <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>=50 km s<sup>-1</sup> Mpc<sup>-1</sup>, \u03a9<sub>0</sub>=1 and \u039b<sub>0</sub>=0.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562868 id=\"20562868\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss1>2 Observations</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">ERO J003707 was first detected in <em>HST</em><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> imaging of A 68 as part of our lensing survey of X-ray luminous galaxy clusters (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001a</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">S02a</a>; Smith et al., in preparation \u2013 S02b). A 68 was observed with <em>HST</em>/WFPC2 for 7.5 ks through the F702W filter and 8.8 ks using UFTI on the 3.8-m United Kingdom Infrared Telescope<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> (UKIRT). We show a subset of these data in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a> and identify the three images of ERO J003707. We refer the reader to S02a and S02b for further details of these data and their reduction.</p>                        <a id=\"20562870\" scrollto-destination=\"20562870\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig001\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">1</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=L~XyZa2ApNW0NFiqgHDGlgY6kT4SAc2yLFnuaGGY1JJ61cogWv0ay~DLklrmcG6LS6pfucdBJn3sEn~K~ZDxPObMXb8pCVujsGd7WcMJCP2S6zN1nI-hmGxuPPCjzDU7dHcrrChpHD51fKmZtawBRU6RQnj4A9TtpLwiTcDjyB25EitT93UqnsuYxzl23eIWc9toRD9ZXl1CjdoKO2L9ucMXCF5I-Nr5093OXCtTBIQLijchkwleZ5QSB7UOqc~LSjYuYhq14loIfO0PF0Hdb2yqOmPYfKf0Iy~jr32sV8xKt9vpRGDbOtORdobfO~mY~m0nD2wM3lcRyABESpH-bQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"True colour RK view (50\u00d750 arcsec2) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical HST and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/20562870/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"20562870\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&Signature=j9eYTzaXzj6AsplcMWXbGcAxquKMr9CRDRcH-Ls8E~YKSvNv7kngKzmIcrF1zImkvzQpHAg6xqupDWzIqM8aCMf99tXdLZf0HqxcL6FlNH1MRWq1wLrtNsOnQ7PWLRE3PUTpzWEyFjUTWK-tETWYMiYeAt-vZ0ukTwpL6wYI9AvEYTZtSJgpnKuWHyGUAUaBo2H6wneSAj6LB4BQeNBh20vlHmFuIicc3TQkKCaDXe6QmkGAqfH82mdh-b8sBXiSHy-ZBf-i~fCH-lHCf14veu~kSmXal68XkIjI1ymO0ik4Ntab4m6odQFYLlwQDZRqVNwhZft8QASIVwsbplMGOw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=20562870&ar=1188318&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">True colour <em>RK</em> view (50\u00d750 arcsec<sup>2</sup>) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical <em>HST</em> and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. The bright elliptical galaxy in the centre of the frame is the central galaxy of the cluster. Three images of ERO J003707 are clearly visible and are marked A, B and C. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig001\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">1</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=L~XyZa2ApNW0NFiqgHDGlgY6kT4SAc2yLFnuaGGY1JJ61cogWv0ay~DLklrmcG6LS6pfucdBJn3sEn~K~ZDxPObMXb8pCVujsGd7WcMJCP2S6zN1nI-hmGxuPPCjzDU7dHcrrChpHD51fKmZtawBRU6RQnj4A9TtpLwiTcDjyB25EitT93UqnsuYxzl23eIWc9toRD9ZXl1CjdoKO2L9ucMXCF5I-Nr5093OXCtTBIQLijchkwleZ5QSB7UOqc~LSjYuYhq14loIfO0PF0Hdb2yqOmPYfKf0Iy~jr32sV8xKt9vpRGDbOtORdobfO~mY~m0nD2wM3lcRyABESpH-bQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"True colour RK view (50\u00d750 arcsec2) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical HST and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/20562870/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"20562870\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&Signature=j9eYTzaXzj6AsplcMWXbGcAxquKMr9CRDRcH-Ls8E~YKSvNv7kngKzmIcrF1zImkvzQpHAg6xqupDWzIqM8aCMf99tXdLZf0HqxcL6FlNH1MRWq1wLrtNsOnQ7PWLRE3PUTpzWEyFjUTWK-tETWYMiYeAt-vZ0ukTwpL6wYI9AvEYTZtSJgpnKuWHyGUAUaBo2H6wneSAj6LB4BQeNBh20vlHmFuIicc3TQkKCaDXe6QmkGAqfH82mdh-b8sBXiSHy-ZBf-i~fCH-lHCf14veu~kSmXal68XkIjI1ymO0ik4Ntab4m6odQFYLlwQDZRqVNwhZft8QASIVwsbplMGOw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=20562870&ar=1188318&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">True colour <em>RK</em> view (50\u00d750 arcsec<sup>2</sup>) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical <em>HST</em> and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. The bright elliptical galaxy in the centre of the frame is the central galaxy of the cluster. Three images of ERO J003707 are clearly visible and are marked A, B and C. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Our near-infrared spectroscopy targeted specific spectral features based on the likely nature of ERO J003707, and a crude estimate of its redshift. We first examined S02a's <em>RIJK</em>-band photometry of ERO J003707 and found that it does not discriminate between old stars and dust-obscuration as the origin of the extreme colours of this galaxy. For simplicity, we therefore fitted an elliptical galaxy spectral template (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Coleman, Wu &amp; Weedman 1980</a>) to S02a's photometry of the central red component using <sub>hyper-z</sub> (Bolzonella, Miralles &amp; Pell\u00f3 1999) and obtained <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u223c1.7. Assuming that the near-infrared emission is dominated by old stars, we searched for the 4000 \u00c5 break and Ca <sub>ii</sub> H and K absorption lines in the <em>z</em> band. However, since S02a's photometry is not conclusive, and ERO J003707 appears to be a disc galaxy (Section 4), we also observed in the <em>J</em>- and <em>H</em>-bands, searching for H<em>\u03b2</em>, [O <sub>iii</sub>], H<em>\u03b1</em> and [N <sub>ii</sub>] emission lines in addition to [O <sub>ii</sub>] which should appear in the <em>z</em> band if ERO J003707 is a star-forming galaxy at <em>z</em>\u223c1.7.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We observed ERO J003707 with CGS4 (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Mountain et al. 1990</a>) on UKIRT on 2001 September 15\u201318 in non-photometric conditions and \u223c1-arcsec seeing with the slit centred on the <em>K</em>-band emission from ERO J003707+0909.5 [<em>\u03b1</em>, <em>\u03b4</em>(J2000)= 003707.37+090928.4]. We adopted a slit position angle of 22\u00b0 to ensure that the <em>K</em>-band emission from image B (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a>) also fell on the slit, although this meant that the disc light was not well sampled. Nodding along the slit, we obtained total integrations of 7.2 and 21.6 ks in the <em>J</em>- and <em>H</em>-bands respectively. These data were reduced in a standard manner using <sub>iraf</sub> tasks. The final reduced frames contained no strong spectral features, although a weak continuum was detected in the <em>H</em> band. We also used the Keck-II 10-m telescope<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a> to obtain <em>z</em>- and <em>H</em>-band spectra of ERO J003707+0909.5 with NIRSPEC (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">McLean et al. 1998</a>) on 2001 November 20\u201321. Using the same target co-ordinates and slit position angle, we integrated for 7.2 and 3.6 ks in the <em>z</em> and <em>H</em> bands respectively, in clear and photometric conditions (FWHM\u223c0.8 arcsec). These data were also reduced in a standard manner using <sub>iraf</sub> tasks, including the <sub>wmkonspec</sub> package. These data confirm the absence of strong emission lines in the <em>H</em> band. The <em>z</em>-band data also contain no strong emission lines, however they reveal a spectral break at 1.04\u00b10.01 \u03bcm. We show the <em>z</em>-band continuum in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig002\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig002\" data-open=\"fig002\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 2</a>. Given the absence of strong emission lines in the <em>zJH</em> bands and the fact that our observations primarily sample the central red component of this galaxy, we interpret the break in the <em>z</em>-band spectrum of ERO J003707 as the 4000 \u00c5 break at a redshift of <em>z</em>=1.60\u00b10.03.</p>                        <a id=\"20562873\" scrollto-destination=\"20562873\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig002\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">2</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig002.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=c2tQg-xVL-O3YHMluqc25Zi-LMoYVVO~D1pH6c9X5OnbeBHytuU8F-6nFUozcKYOePArOD6Gm5~TgYnw0KWA-S1BpHcHASXZsCny9JTzlor2B9eZiFH5lBJcWOJfNTYc3k-JeRyFl6BtCawgo38ksp1NH8WNIPd0o--f~BUKjHaEr01N7a5t-1XwUq5X-CjbyDPQnSuzRjgwWvgpmNLhiXr~BaOSSgxXFcABEMLj8u80N57FONtxDF4~2bCR0OMW6~CWHPOktTW~AyK8GeQPGb12RnuCXqVpkEqlxnwsRha9vwnULo3W26bd~oSkRJC48m~ZRmpj8xld~H3MSm2WrQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The NIRSPEC z-band continuum of the central red bulge of ERO J003707, produced by median filtering the spectrum, rejecting pixels dominated by strong night-sky emission i.e. those regions above the horizontal line in the lower spectrum, which shows the night sky spectrum (offset and scaled for clarity). (2001)</a>.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562875 id=\"20562875\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss2>3 Gravitational lens modelling</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The detailed construction of the lens model of A 68 is described by S02b; here we summarize its key features. The model contains a single lens plane at the cluster redshift (<em>z</em>=0.255) comprising 36 mass components: the cluster dark matter halo centred on the central galaxy; a second dark matter halo centred on the brightest galaxy of a group lying \u223c80 arcsec north-west of the cluster centre; and 34 cluster galaxies including the central galaxy. Each component is described by the parameters: position, ellipticity, orientation, core radius (<em>r</em><sub>core</sub>), cut-off radius (<em>r</em><sub>cut</sub>) and central velocity dispersion (<em>\u03c3</em><sub>o</sub>). The analytical expression used to describe each component is a smoothly truncated pseudo-isothermal elliptical mass distribution (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kneib et al. 1996 \u2013 K96</a>). The centre, ellipticity and orientation of each mass component is matched to the observed light distribution of the related cluster elliptical. The dynamical parameters (<em>r</em><sub>core</sub>, <em>r</em><sub>cut</sub> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub>o</sub>) of the main cluster dark matter halo and the central velocity dispersion of the N-W dark matter halo and the central galaxy are kept as free parameters. The remaining dynamical parameters are scaled with the luminosity of their associated galaxy following K96.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The model parameters were constrained using the three images of the central red region of ERO J003707, as the appearance of these three images in the <em>K</em>-band frame allows us to unambiguously identify these as images of the same underlying region of the galaxy. We use a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> estimator to quantify how well our lens model fits the observational data (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">K96</a>). This estimator is minimised (<em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup>\u223c1) by varying the parameters of the model.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562878 id=\"20562878\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss3>4 Source-plane reconstruction and analysis</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We use our lens model to reconstruct the source-plane properties of ERO J003707. We threshold the <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band frames at 1.5<em>\u03c3</em> above the sky background and ray-trace each pixel to the source plane at <em>z</em>=1.6, thus creating <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band source-plane maps of ERO J003707. We show, as an example, the source-plane reconstruction of ERO J003707+0909.5 (image \u2018A\u2019 from <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a>) in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 3(b)</a>. We also simulate blank-field observations of ERO J003707 (i.e. without a magnifying gravitational lens) by adding the source reconstructions to noise maps from blank sky regions in the original science frames (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Figs 3c and d</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"20562881\" scrollto-destination=\"20562881\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig003\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">3</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig003.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=WqaSONIoiJyXWL3LGmXV1gwt3A9dLZdHn4Mwgc7rZ9dSbZ1Tnhym96tVcRvLnZXZs5~o~0NoOR8qjhDY-JzXTc~S7YIF~eWYsHmpfd3NO6pY5FWYNCYjhYHXwmUJVWgYLmWa-ZrG07vgLc3W4N4vgm-G7Jc~YShWcTFBNsff3UC3l8hS3RzoZGbrseno~XiZUAbuULz9LFPMzTHdl15JC7lsyhvxLdjkFpNMZIQcNLoV9EwLvwA4naq08JVbPJ3Vrrz6izppqdTafp0tnAml69lSyTaR2iB3evAQFyVslNr6NunhDq7fu94eyOfFCChy5GVsTX~-b9RaENF1xBwYuA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"We illustrate the morphology of ERO J003707 using the most strongly amplified of its three gravitational images \u2013 image A from Fig. 1. (a) The image-plane morphology, showing the observed F702W morphology (FWHM\u223c0.15 arcsec) as the grey-scale and the observed <em>K</em>-band morphology (FWHM\u223c0.4 arcsec) as contours. The light from the central galaxy of A 68 had been subtracted from both the <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band data in this panel. (b) The reconstructed F702W (grey-scale) and <em>K</em>-band (contours) source-plane morphology of ERO J003707; both reconstructions are displayed at the pixel-scale and resolution of the original <em>HST</em> observations. (c) A simulated blank-field 7.5-ks <em>HST</em>/WFPC2 observation of ERO J003707. (d) A simulated blank-field 8.8-ks UKIRT/UKIRT (FWHM\u223c0.4 arcsec) observation of ERO J003707. Each panel is 6 arcsec on a side and north is up and east in left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">We measure the source-plane (i.e. \u2018un-lensed\u2019) apparent <em>K</em>-band magnitude of ERO J003707: <em>K</em>=19.8\u00b10.1. This translates into <em>M</em><sub><em>K</em></sub>\u223c-25.0 (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Fioc &amp; Rocca-Volmerange 1997</a>), which suggests that the <em>K</em>-band luminosity of ERO J003707 is comparable to a present day <em>L</em><sup>\u2605</sup> galaxy (adopting <img class=\"contentFigures lazy inline-graphic\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-ieq001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=0VZ78gkdZkK-r4p-sLVwpMb1tQodM-bKuCbWM4cZrKIVJeM-crB0fHFYexWuvJRr-Mi-mlwmegmHjscSKIGqYfgGuUarFhfAxRxj3I7rCCDsRY1KzwjpUitWFFYjowVM5QhNGXX2jOa11viiFur2lueOOtEAoJ1pPG8Wf~59wq6La5LO23Q1bO-glQip9saCR5YVhoA0UjzDuCj6ZuRakNkSeTa7iG-uAkmIntvDaPrQdpceIGjOqY1dYHHvbXOcxyqZ-NAwx2M0RMAnuNU85vHLepwEXLjlwBVrkg84NngdBJt4LRpfngjY8Fs7Q6DceKbBHY1W6UWBAdOaT1sMNg__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"graphic\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-ieq001.jpeg\" /> from <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib8\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib8\" data-open=\"bib8\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Cole et al. 2001</a>). We also measure the (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colour, using an aperture equivalent to a 2-arcsec diameter in the source-plane. We transform the F702W photometry to Cousins <em>R</em> band and correct for interstellar extinction (see <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">S02a</a> for details), to give (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)=5.4\u00b10.1. This is \u223c1 mag bluer than measured by S02a because they did not correct their photometric aperture for lens amplification. S02a therefore measured the colour of the central red bulge in contrast to our photometry that also samples the rest-frame ultraviolet emission from the disc. We also compare our photometry with spectral energy distributions of local galaxies using <sub>pegase</sub> (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib16\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib16\" data-open=\"bib16\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Fioc &amp; Rocca-Volmerange 1997</a>), and find that if a present-day Sa galaxy were placed at <em>z</em>=1.6, it would have a similar (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colour to ERO J003707.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The <em>HST</em> frame reveals a complex and irregular rest-frame ultraviolet (\u223c2700 \u00c5) morphology, comprising 5\u20136 knots indicating current or recent star formation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 3a</a>). In contrast, the <em>K</em>-band morphology consists of a single centrally concentrated component, although there is some diffuse, low surface brightness <em>K</em>-band flux coincident with the knots of rest-frame ultraviolet emission (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 3a</a>). Together with our spectroscopy (Section 2) and the robustness of S02a's classification of ERO J003707 as an ERO after correction for gravitational lensing, this suggests that ERO J003707 is a disc galaxy with a passively evolving central bulge and a modestly star-forming disc. We quantify the morphology by measuring the source-plane concentration (<em>C</em>) and asymmetry (<em>A</em>) (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib1\" data-open=\"bib1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Abraham et al. 1996</a>) of the rest-frame ultraviolet and <em>I</em>-band emission from ERO J003707 using our <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band data. We estimate: <em>C</em>(2700 \u00c5)=0.13\u00b10.02, <em>C</em>(8500 \u00c5)=0.59\u00b10.09,<em>A</em>(2700 \u00c5)=0.79\u00b10.05 and <em>A</em>(8500 \u00c5)=0.56\u00b10.04. These measurements place ERO J003707 in the same region of <em>C</em>\u2013<em>A</em> space as early-type spiral-galaxies in the local Universe when observed at ultraviolet and optical wavelengths (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib6\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib6\" data-open=\"bib6\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Burgarella et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib24\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib24\" data-open=\"bib24\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kuchinski et al. 2001</a>). We also measure the effective radius of the central bulge (<em>r</em><sub>e</sub>) and the exponential scale-length of the disc (<em>r</em><sub>d</sub>). At <em>z</em>=1.6, the 4000 \u00c5 break falls in the <em>z</em> band (Section 2) and so our <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band observations are dominated by the stellar disc and the central bulge respectively. We therefore use our <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band source-plane reconstructions (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 3b</a>) to estimate <em>r</em><sub>e</sub>\u223c0.6 kpc and <em>r</em><sub>d</sub>\u223c3.4 kpc. We compare these estimates with the observed <em>r</em><sub>e</sub>\u2013<em>r</em><sub>d</sub> correlation for early-type disc galaxies in the local Universe (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib22\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib22\" data-open=\"bib22\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Khosroshahi et al. 2000</a>), and find that ERO J003707 is consistent with this correlation. Finally, we estimate that the rest-frame <em>I</em>-band bulge-to-total luminosity ratio, obtaining B/T\u223c0.80\u2013 confirming that ERO J003707 is a bulge-dominated galaxy.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">In summary, our source-plane photometric and morphological analyses reveal that ERO J003707 is similar to an <em>L</em><sup>\u2605</sup> Sa galaxy from the local Universe that is observed at a look-back-time of \u223c10<sup>10</sup> years.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562886 id=\"20562886\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss4>5 Diversity of passive EROs</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The extremely red colour of ERO J003707 is dominated by a central bulge of old stars, however ERO J003707 also appears to contain a weakly star-forming disc. Contrary to the popular assumption (Section 1), passive EROs therefore appear to comprise both elliptical galaxies and early-type spiral-galaxies at <em>z</em>\u22731 (Sa-EROs). Such Sa-EROs have probably not been identified in conventional blank-field ERO surveys (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Daddi et al. 2000</a>) due to the low surface brightness of the disc in these systems \u2013 the disc is only detected at \u22722<em>\u03c3</em> in our simulated blank-field observation of ERO J003707.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We attempt to constrain the surface density of Sa-EROs. Our analysis of ERO J003707 reveals a colour gradient (Section 4); we therefore search for this signature in the other 59 members of S02a's sample. We first quantify the expected gradient using our simulated <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band blank-field observations of ERO J003707 to measure its seeing-matched (FWHM\u223c0.4 arcsec) 1- and 2-arcsec diameter aperture (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colours. Although the 1-arcsec colour is 0.6\u00b10.3 mag redder than the 2-arcsec colour, the two colours are formally consistent within the uncertainties. We repeat this measurement ten times, each time using simulated images that incorporate different noise maps. These experiments confirm the \u22722<em>\u03c3</em> significance of the colour gradient in our simulated observations. We measure the 1- and 2-arcsec diameter aperture (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colours of S02a's ERO sample. We find that four EROs display bluer colours at larger radii consistent with the simulations described above and we note that S02a classified all four of these galaxies as having an irregular morphology. We therefore suggest that \u223c10 per cent of EROs with (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3 and <em>K</em>\u226421 may be Sa-EROs, which translates into a surface density of \u223c0.2 arcmin<sup>\u22122</sup>, based on S02a's counts.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562892 id=\"20562892\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss5>6 Evolution of ERO J003707</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We speculate on the evolution of ERO J003707. The rest-frame <em>I</em>-band luminosity of the central bulge is \u223c4.3\u00d710<sup>10</sup> L<sub>\u2299</sub>. The strong 4000 \u00c5 break (Section 2) suggests that the bulge stars in ERO J003707 formed a few billion years prior to the epoch of observation (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Dunlop et al. 1996</a>). We estimate the age of these stars using the D<sub>4000</sub> index. We measure D<sub>4000</sub>=2.4\u00b10.5, which translates into an age of \u22652.5 Gyr (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib31\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib31\" data-open=\"bib31\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Poggianti &amp; Barbaro 1997</a>; assuming solar metallicity), implying a formation redshift of <em>z</em><sub>f</sub>\u22733.7 in a \u039bCDM cosmology, and <em>z</em><sub>f</sub>\u22736.5 in a standard CDM cosmology (Section 1). This suggests that the bulge was formed in a short, but intense starburst at <em>z</em><sub>f</sub>\u226b2. This event would have certainly disrupted and may have destroyed any existing disc. In contrast to its observed morphology, ERO J003707 was therefore probably a discless galaxy at <em>z</em>\u223c3.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">Turning to the disc component, we estimate the rest-frame <em>I</em>-band disc luminosity to be \u223c7.5\u00d710<sup>9</sup> L<sub>\u2299</sub> and the current star formation rate (SFR) to be SFR\u223c6 M<sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>-1</sup> (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib21\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib21\" data-open=\"bib21\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kennicutt's 1998</a>; assuming a <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib32\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib32\" data-open=\"bib32\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Salpeter (1955)</a> IMF integrated over 0.1\u2013100 M<sub>\u2299</sub> and neglecting dust). Ignoring mergers, the future evolution of ERO J003707 will depend on the duration of star formation in its disc. If the gas becomes exhausted or expelled from the galaxy, then the star formation would cease and ERO J003707 would probably evolve into an E/S0 galaxy by the present day. On the other hand, semi-analytic models of galaxy formation (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Baugh, Cole &amp; Frenk 1996</a>) predict that bulges and spheroids are formed from merging of disc galaxies and subsequently may accrete gas, thus re-growing a disc component. We explore this \u2018transvestite galaxy\u2019 hypothesis (Richard Bower, private communication) by estimating the time-scale (<em>\u03c4</em><sub>eq</sub>) over which, at the current SFR, the disc stellar mass will become comparable with the bulge stellar mass. We convert the bulge luminosity to a stellar mass (assuming <em>M</em>/<em>L</em>\u223c1;<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib4\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib4\" data-open=\"bib4\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Bell &amp; de Jong 2001</a>) and divide by the disc SFR to obtain <em>\u03c4</em><sub>eq</sub>\u223c6\u00d710<sup>9</sup> yrs which is roughly equal to the look-back-time from the present day to <em>z</em>=1.6. This raises the intriguing possibility that the progenitors of a fraction of luminous spiral galaxies in the local Universe could have been EROs when observed at <em>z</em>\u22731.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562897 id=\"20562897\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss6>7 Conclusions</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We present near-infrared spectroscopy and optical <em>HST</em> imaging of ERO J003707+0909.5, the brightest of three gravitationally lensed images of ERO J003707 in the field of the massive cluster A 68 (<em>z</em>=0.255). Our near-infrared spectroscopy reveals a break at <em>\u03bb</em><sub>obs</sub>=1.04\u00b10.01 \u03bcm in the spectral energy distribution of this galaxy. This feature arises from the redshifted 4000 \u00c5 break of a centrally concentrated old stellar population, which places ERO J003707 at <em>z</em>=1.60\u00b10.03. We constrain a detailed model of the cluster lens and then reconstruct the source-plane properties of ERO J003707. Our main conclusions are as follows.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\"></p><ul class=\"list-simple\"><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">(i) The luminosity and (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colour of ERO J003707 are similar to those of an <em>L</em><sup>\u2605</sup> galaxy with a SFR comparable to an Sa galaxy in the local Universe.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">(ii) Morphological parameters (<em>C</em>, <em>A</em>, <em>r</em><sub>e</sub>, <em>r</em><sub>d</sub>, <em>B</em>/<em>T</em>) based on rest-frame ultraviolet and <em>I</em>-band emission confirm that ERO J003707 is an early-type disc galaxy.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">(iii) The unique association of passive EROs with elliptical galaxies is clearly too simplistic. We estimate that \u223c10 per cent of EROs with (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3 and <em>K</em>\u226421 may be Sa-EROs i.e. early-type disc galaxies similar to ERO J003707.</p></li><li><p class=\"chapter-para\">(iv) We estimate that the bulge stars in ERO J003707 formed \u22652.5 Gyr ago and speculate that, if the current disc SFR (\u223c6 M<sub>\u2299</sub> yr<sup>\u22121</sup>) continues due to the cooling of gas onto ERO J003707 in the manner predicted by hierarchical galaxy formation models (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib3\" data-open=\"bib3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Baugh et al. 1996</a>), then ERO J003707 would evolve into a luminous spiral galaxy by the present day.</p></li></ul>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562906 id=\"20562906\" class=\"backacknowledgements-title js-splitscreen-backacknowledgements-title\" data-legacy-id=ss7>Acknowledgments</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">GPS thanks Richard Bower for his encouragement and Olga Kuhn for her assistance with the UKIRT/CGS4 observations. We are grateful to Carlton Baugh, Arjun Dey, Alastair Edge and Rob Ivison for helpful discussions and assistance. We acknowledge an anonymous referee for exceptionally prompt feedback. We also thank Andy Adamson, John Davies and Tom Kerr for their support of the UKIRT observing programme. GPS acknowledges a postgraduate studentship from PPARC. IRS acknowledges support from the Royal Society and a Philip Leverhulme Prize Fellowship. JPK acknowledges support from CNRS. OC acknowledges support from the European Commission under contract no. ER-BFM-BICT97- 2471. HE acknowledges support by NASA and STScI grants NAG 5-6336 and GO 5-08249. We also acknowledge support from the UK-French ALLIANCE collaboration programme #00161XM.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562910 id=\"20562910\" class=\"backreferences-title js-splitscreen-backreferences-title\" >References</h2>\r\n<div class=\"ref-list js-splitview-ref-list\"><div content-id=\"bib1\" class=\"js-splitview-ref-item\" data-legacy-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"refLink-parent\"><span class=\"refLink\"><a name=\"jumplink-bib1\" href=\"javascript:;\" aria-label=\"jumplink-bib1\" data-id=\"\"></a></span></div><div class=\"ref false\"><div id=\"ref-auto-bib1\" class=\"ref-content \" data-id=\"bib1\"><div class=\"mixed-citation citation\"><p class=\"mixed-citation-compatibility\">\r\n<span class=\"person-group\">\r\n<div class=\"name\"><div class=\"surname\">Abraham</div> <div class=\"given-names\">R. type=\"hidden\" value=\"mnras\" /> \r\n    </div>\r\n\r\n                    </div>\r\n                </div>\r\n            </div>\r\n            <div id=\"ContentTab\" class=\"content active\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget widget-ArticleFulltext widget-instance-OUP_Article_FullText_Widget\">\r\n        <div class=\"module-widget\">\r\n    <div class=\"widget-items\" data-widgetname=\"ArticleFulltext\">\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562862 id=\"20562862\" class=\"abstract-title js-splitscreen-abstract-title\" >Abstract</h2>\r\n<section class=\"abstract\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">We present near-infrared spectroscopy and <em>Hubble Space Telescope (HST)</em> imaging of galaxy J003707+0909.5, the brightest of three gravitationally lensed images of an extremely red object (ERO) at <em>z</em>=1.6, in the field of the massive cluster A 68 (<em>z</em>=0.255). We exploit the superlative resolution of our <em>HST</em> data and the enhanced spatial resolution and sensitivity afforded by the lens amplification to reconstruct the source-plane properties of this ERO. Our morphological and photometric analysis reveals that ERO J003707 is an <em>L</em><sup>\u2605</sup> early-type disc galaxy and we estimate that \u223c10 per cent of galaxies with (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3 and <em>K</em>\u226421 may have similar properties. The unique association of passive EROs with elliptical galaxies therefore appears to be too simplistic. We speculate on the evolution of ERO J003707: if gas continues to cool on to this galaxy in the manner predicted by hierarchical galaxy formation models, then by the present day, ERO J003707 could evolve into a very luminous spiral galaxy.</p></section>                        <div class=\"article-metadata-panel clearfix at-ArticleMetadata\"></div>\r\n<div class=\"kwd-group\"><a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;gravitational lensing&quot;\">gravitational lensing</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: clusters: individual: A 68&quot;\">galaxies: clusters: individual: A 68</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: evolution&quot;\">galaxies: evolution</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: high-redshift&quot;\">galaxies: high-redshift</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: individual: ERO J003707+0909.5&quot;\">galaxies: individual: ERO J003707+0909.5</a>, <a class=\"kwd-part kwd-main\" href=\"javascript:;\" data-keyword=\"&quot;galaxies: spiral&quot;\">galaxies: spiral</a></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562864 id=\"20562864\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss0>1 Introduction</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The cores of massive galaxy clusters act as powerful gravitational lenses, providing a magnified view of serendipitously placed distant galaxies. Indeed, close alignment of a background galaxy behind a cluster lens can lead to multiple images being visible. This phenomenon, known as strong lensing, has been applied on numerous occasions to the study of high-redshift galaxies (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib19\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib19\" data-open=\"bib19\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Hammer &amp; Rigaut 1989</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib13\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib13\" data-open=\"bib13\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Ebbels et al. 1996</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib18\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib18\" data-open=\"bib18\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Franx et al. 1997</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib33\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib33\" data-open=\"bib33\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Seitz et al. 1998</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib29\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib29\" data-open=\"bib29\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Pell\u00f3 et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib15\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib15\" data-open=\"bib15\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Ellis et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001a</a>). A key feature of these studies is that the lens magnification enables intrinsically low-luminosity galaxies at remote epochs to be observed and studied in great detail.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">One class of distant galaxy that may benefit from the enhanced sensitivity provided by a gravitational lens is extremely red objects (EROs). The defining optical/near-infrared colours of EROs, (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>)\u22655.3, should select either distant elliptical galaxies (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib12\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib12\" data-open=\"bib12\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Dunlop et al. 1996</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib37\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib37\" data-open=\"bib37\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Soifer et al. 1999</a>) or heavily dust-obscured systems at <em>z</em>\u223c1\u20132 (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib11\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib11\" data-open=\"bib11\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Dey et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib30\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib30\" data-open=\"bib30\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Pierre et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib2\" data-open=\"bib2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Afonso et al. 2001</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib35\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib35\" data-open=\"bib35\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001b</a>). Here we concentrate on those EROs whose (<em>R</em>-<em>K</em>) colour is dominated by the 4000-\u00c5 break of an evolved stellar population (i.e. \u2018passive EROs\u2019). Various authors (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib20\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib20\" data-open=\"bib20\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kauffmann &amp; Charlot 1998</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib17\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib17\" data-open=\"bib17\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Fontana et al. 1999</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib10\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib10\" data-open=\"bib10\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Daddi et al. 2000</a>) have used observations of passive EROs and other <em>K</em>-selected samples to argue for or against monolithic collapse (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib14\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib14\" data-open=\"bib14\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Eggen, Lynden-Bell &amp; Sandage 1962</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib25\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib25\" data-open=\"bib25\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Larson 1975</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib38\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib38\" data-open=\"bib38\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Tinsley &amp; Gunn 1976</a>) or hierarchical assembly (e.g. <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib39\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib39\" data-open=\"bib39\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">White &amp; Frenk 1991</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib7\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib7\" data-open=\"bib7\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Cole et al. 2000</a>) theories of galaxy formation. Regardless of the details of this debate, both arguments implicitly assume that <em>all</em> passive EROs are elliptical galaxies.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The importance of EROs to our understanding of galaxy formation and the intrinsic faintness of these systems (<em>R</em>\u227323, <em>K</em>\u227318) recently motivated us to search for EROs in the fields of massive gravitational lenses (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2002\u2013 S02a</a>). In this letter, we present near-infrared spectroscopy of ERO J003707+0909.5, drawn from S02a's sample. We describe our observations in Section 2, present our lens modelling in Section 3, describe our source-plane analysis in Section 4, discuss the diversity of passive EROs in Section 5, speculate on the evolution of ERO J003707 in Section 6 and finally summarize our conclusions in Section 7. We assume <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>=50 km s<sup>-1</sup> Mpc<sup>-1</sup>, \u03a9<sub>0</sub>=1 and \u039b<sub>0</sub>=0.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562868 id=\"20562868\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss1>2 Observations</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">ERO J003707 was first detected in <em>HST</em><span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn1\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn1\" data-open=\"fn1\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>1</sup></a> imaging of A 68 as part of our lensing survey of X-ray luminous galaxy clusters (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib34\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib34\" data-open=\"bib34\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Smith et al. 2001a</a>; <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib36\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib36\" data-open=\"bib36\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">S02a</a>; Smith et al., in preparation \u2013 S02b). A 68 was observed with <em>HST</em>/WFPC2 for 7.5 ks through the F702W filter and 8.8 ks using UFTI on the 3.8-m United Kingdom Infrared Telescope<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn2\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn2\" data-open=\"fn2\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>2</sup></a> (UKIRT). We show a subset of these data in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a> and identify the three images of ERO J003707. We refer the reader to S02a and S02b for further details of these data and their reduction.</p>                        <a id=\"20562870\" scrollto-destination=\"20562870\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig001\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">1</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=L~XyZa2ApNW0NFiqgHDGlgY6kT4SAc2yLFnuaGGY1JJ61cogWv0ay~DLklrmcG6LS6pfucdBJn3sEn~K~ZDxPObMXb8pCVujsGd7WcMJCP2S6zN1nI-hmGxuPPCjzDU7dHcrrChpHD51fKmZtawBRU6RQnj4A9TtpLwiTcDjyB25EitT93UqnsuYxzl23eIWc9toRD9ZXl1CjdoKO2L9ucMXCF5I-Nr5093OXCtTBIQLijchkwleZ5QSB7UOqc~LSjYuYhq14loIfO0PF0Hdb2yqOmPYfKf0Iy~jr32sV8xKt9vpRGDbOtORdobfO~mY~m0nD2wM3lcRyABESpH-bQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"True colour RK view (50\u00d750 arcsec2) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical HST and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. North is up and east is left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/20562870/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"20562870\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&Signature=j9eYTzaXzj6AsplcMWXbGcAxquKMr9CRDRcH-Ls8E~YKSvNv7kngKzmIcrF1zImkvzQpHAg6xqupDWzIqM8aCMf99tXdLZf0HqxcL6FlNH1MRWq1wLrtNsOnQ7PWLRE3PUTpzWEyFjUTWK-tETWYMiYeAt-vZ0ukTwpL6wYI9AvEYTZtSJgpnKuWHyGUAUaBo2H6wneSAj6LB4BQeNBh20vlHmFuIicc3TQkKCaDXe6QmkGAqfH82mdh-b8sBXiSHy-ZBf-i~fCH-lHCf14veu~kSmXal68XkIjI1ymO0ik4Ntab4m6odQFYLlwQDZRqVNwhZft8QASIVwsbplMGOw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=20562870&ar=1188318&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">True colour <em>RK</em> view (50\u00d750 arcsec<sup>2</sup>) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical <em>HST</em> and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. The bright elliptical galaxy in the centre of the frame is the central galaxy of the cluster. Three images of ERO J003707 are clearly visible and are marked A, B and C. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.</p></div></div><div content-id=\"fig001\" class=\"fig fig-modal reveal-modal\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">1</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=L~XyZa2ApNW0NFiqgHDGlgY6kT4SAc2yLFnuaGGY1JJ61cogWv0ay~DLklrmcG6LS6pfucdBJn3sEn~K~ZDxPObMXb8pCVujsGd7WcMJCP2S6zN1nI-hmGxuPPCjzDU7dHcrrChpHD51fKmZtawBRU6RQnj4A9TtpLwiTcDjyB25EitT93UqnsuYxzl23eIWc9toRD9ZXl1CjdoKO2L9ucMXCF5I-Nr5093OXCtTBIQLijchkwleZ5QSB7UOqc~LSjYuYhq14loIfO0PF0Hdb2yqOmPYfKf0Iy~jr32sV8xKt9vpRGDbOtORdobfO~mY~m0nD2wM3lcRyABESpH-bQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"True colour RK view (50\u00d750 arcsec2) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical HST and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" /><div class=\"fig-orig original-slide\"><a class=\"fig-view-orig js-view-large openInAnotherWindow\" href=\"/view-large/figure/20562870/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\" target=\"_blank\">Open in new tab</a><a data-section=\"20562870\" href=\"/DownloadFile/DownloadImage.aspx?image=https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&Signature=j9eYTzaXzj6AsplcMWXbGcAxquKMr9CRDRcH-Ls8E~YKSvNv7kngKzmIcrF1zImkvzQpHAg6xqupDWzIqM8aCMf99tXdLZf0HqxcL6FlNH1MRWq1wLrtNsOnQ7PWLRE3PUTpzWEyFjUTWK-tETWYMiYeAt-vZ0ukTwpL6wYI9AvEYTZtSJgpnKuWHyGUAUaBo2H6wneSAj6LB4BQeNBh20vlHmFuIicc3TQkKCaDXe6QmkGAqfH82mdh-b8sBXiSHy-ZBf-i~fCH-lHCf14veu~kSmXal68XkIjI1ymO0ik4Ntab4m6odQFYLlwQDZRqVNwhZft8QASIVwsbplMGOw__&Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA&sec=20562870&ar=1188318&xsltPath=~/UI/app/XSLT&imagename=&siteId=5326\" class=\"download-slide\" data-path-from-xml=\"333-1-L16-fig001.jpeg\">Download slide</a></div></div><div class=\"caption fig-caption\"><p class=\"chapter-para\">True colour <em>RK</em> view (50\u00d750 arcsec<sup>2</sup>) of the core of the galaxy cluster A 68, exploiting the superlative resolution of both our optical <em>HST</em> and near-infrared UKIRT imaging data. The bright elliptical galaxy in the centre of the frame is the central galaxy of the cluster. Three images of ERO J003707 are clearly visible and are marked A, B and C. Each image comprises a central red \u2018bulge\u2019, surrounded by numerous fainter blue knots of current or recent star formation. North is up and east is left.</p></div></div><p class=\"chapter-para\">Our near-infrared spectroscopy targeted specific spectral features based on the likely nature of ERO J003707, and a crude estimate of its redshift. We first examined S02a's <em>RIJK</em>-band photometry of ERO J003707 and found that it does not discriminate between old stars and dust-obscuration as the origin of the extreme colours of this galaxy. For simplicity, we therefore fitted an elliptical galaxy spectral template (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib9\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib9\" data-open=\"bib9\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Coleman, Wu &amp; Weedman 1980</a>) to S02a's photometry of the central red component using <sub>hyper-z</sub> (Bolzonella, Miralles &amp; Pell\u00f3 1999) and obtained <em>z</em><sub>phot</sub>\u223c1.7. Assuming that the near-infrared emission is dominated by old stars, we searched for the 4000 \u00c5 break and Ca <sub>ii</sub> H and K absorption lines in the <em>z</em> band. However, since S02a's photometry is not conclusive, and ERO J003707 appears to be a disc galaxy (Section 4), we also observed in the <em>J</em>- and <em>H</em>-bands, searching for H<em>\u03b2</em>, [O <sub>iii</sub>], H<em>\u03b1</em> and [N <sub>ii</sub>] emission lines in addition to [O <sub>ii</sub>] which should appear in the <em>z</em> band if ERO J003707 is a star-forming galaxy at <em>z</em>\u223c1.7.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">We observed ERO J003707 with CGS4 (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib28\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib28\" data-open=\"bib28\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Mountain et al. 1990</a>) on UKIRT on 2001 September 15\u201318 in non-photometric conditions and \u223c1-arcsec seeing with the slit centred on the <em>K</em>-band emission from ERO J003707+0909.5 [<em>\u03b1</em>, <em>\u03b4</em>(J2000)= 003707.37+090928.4]. We adopted a slit position angle of 22\u00b0 to ensure that the <em>K</em>-band emission from image B (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a>) also fell on the slit, although this meant that the disc light was not well sampled. Nodding along the slit, we obtained total integrations of 7.2 and 21.6 ks in the <em>J</em>- and <em>H</em>-bands respectively. These data were reduced in a standard manner using <sub>iraf</sub> tasks. The final reduced frames contained no strong spectral features, although a weak continuum was detected in the <em>H</em> band. We also used the Keck-II 10-m telescope<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fn3\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fn3\" data-open=\"fn3\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-fn js-xref-fn\"><sup>3</sup></a> to obtain <em>z</em>- and <em>H</em>-band spectra of ERO J003707+0909.5 with NIRSPEC (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib26\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib26\" data-open=\"bib26\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">McLean et al. 1998</a>) on 2001 November 20\u201321. Using the same target co-ordinates and slit position angle, we integrated for 7.2 and 3.6 ks in the <em>z</em> and <em>H</em> bands respectively, in clear and photometric conditions (FWHM\u223c0.8 arcsec). These data were also reduced in a standard manner using <sub>iraf</sub> tasks, including the <sub>wmkonspec</sub> package. These data confirm the absence of strong emission lines in the <em>H</em> band. The <em>z</em>-band data also contain no strong emission lines, however they reveal a spectral break at 1.04\u00b10.01 \u03bcm. We show the <em>z</em>-band continuum in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig002\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig002\" data-open=\"fig002\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 2</a>. Given the absence of strong emission lines in the <em>zJH</em> bands and the fact that our observations primarily sample the central red component of this galaxy, we interpret the break in the <em>z</em>-band spectrum of ERO J003707 as the 4000 \u00c5 break at a redshift of <em>z</em>=1.60\u00b10.03.</p>                        <a id=\"20562873\" scrollto-destination=\"20562873\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig002\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">2</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig002.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=c2tQg-xVL-O3YHMluqc25Zi-LMoYVVO~D1pH6c9X5OnbeBHytuU8F-6nFUozcKYOePArOD6Gm5~TgYnw0KWA-S1BpHcHASXZsCny9JTzlor2B9eZiFH5lBJcWOJfNTYc3k-JeRyFl6BtCawgo38ksp1NH8WNIPd0o--f~BUKjHaEr01N7a5t-1XwUq5X-CjbyDPQnSuzRjgwWvgpmNLhiXr~BaOSSgxXFcABEMLj8u80N57FONtxDF4~2bCR0OMW6~CWHPOktTW~AyK8GeQPGb12RnuCXqVpkEqlxnwsRha9vwnULo3W26bd~oSkRJC48m~ZRmpj8xld~H3MSm2WrQ__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"The NIRSPEC z-band continuum of the central red bulge of ERO J003707, produced by median filtering the spectrum, rejecting pixels dominated by strong night-sky emission i.e. those regions above the horizontal line in the lower spectrum, which shows the night sky spectrum (offset and scaled for clarity). (2001)</a>.</p></div></div>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562875 id=\"20562875\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss2>3 Gravitational lens modelling</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">The detailed construction of the lens model of A 68 is described by S02b; here we summarize its key features. The model contains a single lens plane at the cluster redshift (<em>z</em>=0.255) comprising 36 mass components: the cluster dark matter halo centred on the central galaxy; a second dark matter halo centred on the brightest galaxy of a group lying \u223c80 arcsec north-west of the cluster centre; and 34 cluster galaxies including the central galaxy. Each component is described by the parameters: position, ellipticity, orientation, core radius (<em>r</em><sub>core</sub>), cut-off radius (<em>r</em><sub>cut</sub>) and central velocity dispersion (<em>\u03c3</em><sub>o</sub>). The analytical expression used to describe each component is a smoothly truncated pseudo-isothermal elliptical mass distribution (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">Kneib et al. 1996 \u2013 K96</a>). The centre, ellipticity and orientation of each mass component is matched to the observed light distribution of the related cluster elliptical. The dynamical parameters (<em>r</em><sub>core</sub>, <em>r</em><sub>cut</sub> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub>o</sub>) of the main cluster dark matter halo and the central velocity dispersion of the N-W dark matter halo and the central galaxy are kept as free parameters. The remaining dynamical parameters are scaled with the luminosity of their associated galaxy following K96.</p><p class=\"chapter-para\">The model parameters were constrained using the three images of the central red region of ERO J003707, as the appearance of these three images in the <em>K</em>-band frame allows us to unambiguously identify these as images of the same underlying region of the galaxy. We use a <em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup> estimator to quantify how well our lens model fits the observational data (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-bib23\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"bib23\" data-open=\"bib23\" class=\"link link-ref link-reveal xref-bibr\">K96</a>). This estimator is minimised (<em>\u03c7</em><sup>2</sup>\u223c1) by varying the parameters of the model.</p>                    <h2 scrollto-destination=20562878 id=\"20562878\" class=\"section-title js-splitscreen-section-title\" data-legacy-id=ss3>4 Source-plane reconstruction and analysis</h2>\r\n<p class=\"chapter-para\">We use our lens model to reconstruct the source-plane properties of ERO J003707. We threshold the <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band frames at 1.5<em>\u03c3</em> above the sky background and ray-trace each pixel to the source plane at <em>z</em>=1.6, thus creating <em>R</em>- and <em>K</em>-band source-plane maps of ERO J003707. We show, as an example, the source-plane reconstruction of ERO J003707+0909.5 (image \u2018A\u2019 from <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig001\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig001\" data-open=\"fig001\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 1</a>) in <span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Fig. 3(b)</a>. We also simulate blank-field observations of ERO J003707 (i.e. without a magnifying gravitational lens) by adding the source reconstructions to noise maps from blank sky regions in the original science frames (<span class=\"xrefLink\" id=\"jumplink-fig003\"></span><a href=\"javascript:;\" reveal-id=\"fig003\" data-open=\"fig003\" class=\"link link-reveal link-table xref-fig\">Figs 3c and d</a>).</p>                        <a id=\"20562881\" scrollto-destination=\"20562881\"></a>\r\n<div data-id=\"fig003\" class=\"fig fig-section js-fig-section\"><div class=\"label fig-label\">3</div><div class=\"graphic-wrap\"><img class=\"content-image\" src=\"https://oup.silverchair-cdn.com/oup/backfile/Content_public/Journal/mnras/333/1/10.1046/j.1365-8711.2002.05501.x/2/m_333-1-L16-fig003.jpeg?Expires=1639265927&amp;Signature=WqaSONIoiJyXWL3LGmXV1gwt3A9dLZdHn4Mwgc7rZ9dSbZ1Tnhym96tVcRvLnZXZs5~o~0NoOR8qjhDY-JzXTc~S7YIF~eWYsHmpfd3NO6pY5FWYNCYjhYHXwmUJVWgYLmWa-ZrG07vgLc3W4N4vgm-G7Jc~YShWcTFBNsff3UC3l8hS3RzoZGbrseno~XiZUAbuULz9LFPMzTHdl15JC7lsyhvxLdjkFpNMZIQcNLoV9EwLvwA4naq08JVbPJ3Vrrz6izppqdTafp0tnAml69lSyTaR2iB3evAQFyVslNr6NunhDq7fu94eyOfFCChy5GVsTX~-b9RaENF1xBwYuA__&amp;Key-Pair-Id=APKAIE5G5CRDK6RD3PGA\" alt=\"We illustrate the morphology of ERO J003707 using the most strongly amplified of its three gravitational images \u2013 image A from Fig. 1.
